     <p>
      A
      <b>
       biofuel
      </b>
      is a
      <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">
       fuel
      </a>
      that is produced through contemporary biological processes, such as agriculture and
      <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion" title="Anaerobic digestion">
       anaerobic digestion
      </a>
      , rather than a fuel produced by geological processes such as those involved in the formation of
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">
       fossil fuels
      </a>
      , such as coal and petroleum, from prehistoric biological matter. Biofuels can be derived directly from plants, or indirectly from agricultural, commercial, domestic, and/or industrial wastes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Renewable biofuels generally involve contemporary
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">
       carbon fixation
      </a>
      , such as those that occur in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">
       plants
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microalgae" title="Microalgae">
       microalgae
      </a>
      through the process of
      <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">
       photosynthesis
      </a>
      . Other renewable biofuels are made through the use or conversion of
      <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">
       biomass
      </a>
      (referring to recently living organisms, most often referring to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">
       plants
      </a>
      or plant-derived materials). This biomass can be converted to convenient energy-containing substances in three different ways: thermal conversion, chemical conversion, and biochemical conversion. This biomass conversion can result in fuel in
      <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">
       solid
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">
       liquid
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">
       gas
      </a>
      form. This new biomass can also be used directly for biofuels.
     </p>
     <p>
      Bioethanol is an
      <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">
       alcohol
      </a>
      made by
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">
       fermentation
      </a>
      , mostly from
      <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">
       carbohydrates
      </a>
      produced in
      <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">
       sugar
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">
       starch
      </a>
      crops such as
      <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">
       corn
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">
       sugarcane
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Sweet_sorghum" title="Sweet sorghum">
       sweet sorghum
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">
       Cellulosic biomass
      </a>
      , derived from non-food sources, such as trees and grasses, is also being developed as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedstock" title="Feedstock">
       feedstock
      </a>
      for ethanol production. Ethanol can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form, but it is usually used as a
      <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">
       gasoline
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_additive" title="Fuel additive">
       additive
      </a>
      to increase octane and improve vehicle emissions. Bioethanol is widely used in the
      <a href="/wiki/Biofuel_in_the_United_States" title="Biofuel in the United States">
       USA
      </a>
      and in
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">
       Brazil
      </a>
      . Current plant design does not provide for converting the
      <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">
       lignin
      </a>
      portion of plant raw materials to fuel components by fermentation.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">
       Biodiesel
      </a>
      can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form, but it is usually used as a
      <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">
       diesel
      </a>
      additive to reduce levels of particulates,
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">
       carbon monoxide
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">
       hydrocarbons
      </a>
      from diesel-powered vehicles. Biodiesel is produced from oils or fats using
      <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">
       transesterification
      </a>
      and is the most common biofuel in Europe.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2010, worldwide biofuel production reached 105 billion liters (28 billion gallons US), up 17% from 2009,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biofuels2010_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Biofuels2010-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and biofuels provided 2.7% of the world's fuels for road transport. Global
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">
       ethanol fuel
      </a>
      production reached 86 billion liters (23 billion gallons US) in 2010, with the United States and Brazil as the world's top producers, accounting together for 90% of global production. The world's largest biodiesel producer is the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
       European Union
      </a>
      , accounting for 53% of all biodiesel production in 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biofuels2010_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Biofuels2010-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of 2011, mandates for blending biofuels exist in 31 countries at the national level and in 29 states or provinces.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren212011_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ren212011-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">
       International Energy Agency
      </a>
      has a goal for biofuels to meet more than a quarter of world demand for transportation fuels by 2050 to reduce dependence on petroleum and coal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The production of biofuels also led into a flourishing automotive industry, where by 2010, 79% of all cars produced in Brazil were made with a hybrid fuel system of bioethanol and gasoline.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There are various social, economic, environmental and technical
      <a href="/wiki/Issues_relating_to_biofuels" title="Issues relating to biofuels">
       issues relating to biofuels
      </a>
      production and use, which have been debated in the popular media and scientific journals. These include: the effect of moderating
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Price_of_petroleum" title="Price of petroleum">
       oil prices
      </a>
      , the "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_vs_fuel" title="Food vs fuel">
       food vs fuel
      </a>
      " debate,
      <a href="/wiki/Poverty_reduction" title="Poverty reduction">
       poverty reduction
      </a>
      potential,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions">
       carbon emissions
      </a>
      levels,
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_biofuel" title="Sustainable biofuel">
       sustainable biofuel
      </a>
      production,
      <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">
       deforestation
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">
       soil erosion
      </a>
      , loss of
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">
       biodiversity
      </a>
      , impact on
      <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">
       water resources
      </a>
      , rural social exclusion and injustice, shantytown migration, rural unskilled unemployment, and nitrous oxide (NO2) emissions.
     </p>

            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </li>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Liquid_fuels_for_transportation">
       Liquid fuels for transportation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Liquid fuels for transportation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This section
         <b>
          needs additional citations for
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           verification
          </a>
         </b>
         .
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          Please help
          <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit">
           improve this article
          </a>
          by
          <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
           adding citations to reliable sources
          </a>
          . Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (June 2016)
          </i>
         </small>
         <small class="hide-when-compact">
          <i>
           (
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
            Learn how and when to remove this template message
           </a>
           )
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Most transportation fuels are liquids, because vehicles usually require high
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">
       energy density
      </a>
      . This occurs naturally in liquids and solids. High energy density can also be provided by an
      <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">
       internal combustion engine
      </a>
      . These engines require clean-burning fuels. The fuels that are easiest to burn cleanly are typically liquids and gases. Thus, liquids meet the requirements of being both energy-dense and clean-burning. In addition, liquids (and gases) can be pumped, which means handling is easily mechanized, and thus less laborious.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="First-generation_biofuels">
       First-generation biofuels
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: First-generation biofuels">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      "First-generation" or conventional biofuels are made from sugar, starch, or vegetable oil.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ethanol">
       Ethanol
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ethanol">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">
       Ethanol fuel
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg/220px-Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg/330px-Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg/440px-Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E100" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">
         Neat ethanol
        </a>
        on the left (A),
        <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">
         gasoline
        </a>
        on the right (G) at a
        <a href="/wiki/Filling_station" title="Filling station">
         filling station
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
         Brazil
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Biologically produced
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohols" title="Alcohols">
       alcohols
      </a>
      , most commonly
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">
       ethanol
      </a>
      , and less commonly
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propan-1-ol" title="Propan-1-ol">
       propanol
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Butanol_fuel" title="Butanol fuel">
       butanol
      </a>
      , are produced by the action of
      <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">
       microorganisms
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">
       enzymes
      </a>
      through the fermentation of sugars or starches (easiest), or cellulose (which is more difficult).
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biobutanol" title="Biobutanol">
       Biobutanol
      </a>
      (also called biogasoline) is often claimed to provide a direct replacement for gasoline, because it can be used directly in a gasoline engine.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bushlulacana09032007.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3506" data-file-width="2337" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bushlulacana09032007.jpg/220px-Bushlulacana09032007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bushlulacana09032007.jpg/330px-Bushlulacana09032007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bushlulacana09032007.jpg/440px-Bushlulacana09032007.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bushlulacana09032007.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        U.S. President
        <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">
         George W. Bush
        </a>
        looks at sugar cane, a source of biofuel, with Brazilian President
        <a href="/wiki/Luiz_In%C3%A1cio_Lula_da_Silva" title="Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva">
         Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva
        </a>
        during a tour on biofuel technology at
        <a href="/wiki/Petrobras" title="Petrobras">
         Petrobras
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">
         São Paulo
        </a>
        , Brazil, 9 March 2007.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">
       Ethanol fuel
      </a>
      is the most common biofuel worldwide, particularly
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">
       in Brazil
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_fuel" title="Alcohol fuel">
       Alcohol fuels
      </a>
      are produced by fermentation of sugars derived from
      <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">
       wheat
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">
       corn
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">
       sugar beets
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">
       sugar cane
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">
       molasses
      </a>
      and any sugar or starch from which
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">
       alcoholic beverages
      </a>
      such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whiskey" title="Whiskey">
       whiskey
      </a>
      , can be made (such as
      <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">
       potato
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">
       fruit
      </a>
      waste, etc.). The ethanol production methods used are
      <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzyme" title="Digestive enzyme">
       enzyme digestion
      </a>
      (to release sugars from stored starches), fermentation of the sugars,
      <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">
       distillation
      </a>
      and drying. The distillation process requires significant energy input for heat (sometimes unsustainable
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">
       natural gas
      </a>
      fossil fuel, but cellulosic biomass such as
      <a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">
       bagasse
      </a>
      , the waste left after sugar cane is pressed to extract its juice, is the most common fuel in Brazil, while pellets, wood chips and also waste heat are more common in Europe)
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.eon.se/om-eon/Om-energi/Produktion-av-el-gas-varme-och-kyla/Kraftvarme/Vara-kraftvarmeverk/Handeloverket/Handeloverket-Panna-15/Energikombinatet_bild/" rel="nofollow">
       Waste steam fuels ethanol factory
      </a>
      - where waste heat from the factories also is used in the district heating grid.
     </p>
     <p>
      Ethanol can be used in petrol engines as a replacement for gasoline; it can be mixed with gasoline to any percentage. Most existing car petrol engines can run on blends of up to 15% bioethanol with petroleum/gasoline. Ethanol has a smaller
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">
       energy density
      </a>
      than that of gasoline; this means it takes more fuel (volume and mass) to produce the same amount of work. An advantage of ethanol (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       CH
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        3
       </span>
       CH
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       OH
      </span>
      ) is that it has a higher
      <a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">
       octane rating
      </a>
      than ethanol-free gasoline available at roadside gas stations, which allows an increase of an engine's
      <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">
       compression ratio
      </a>
      for increased
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_efficiency" title="Thermal efficiency">
       thermal efficiency
      </a>
      . In high-altitude (thin air) locations, some states mandate a mix of gasoline and ethanol as a winter
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidizer" title="Oxidizer">
       oxidizer
      </a>
      to reduce atmospheric pollution emissions.
     </p>
     <p>
      Ethanol is also used to fuel bioethanol
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fireplaces" title="Fireplaces">
       fireplaces
      </a>
      . As they do not require a chimney and are "flueless", bioethanol fires
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      are extremely useful for newly built homes and apartments without a flue. The downsides to these fireplaces is that their heat output is slightly less than electric heat or gas fires, and precautions must be taken to avoid carbon monoxide poisoning.
     </p>
     <p>
      Corn-to-ethanol and other food stocks has led to the development of
      <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">
       cellulosic ethanol
      </a>
      . According to a joint research agenda conducted through the US Department of Energy,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the fossil energy ratios (
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fossil_Energy_Ratio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fossil Energy Ratio (page does not exist)">
       FER
      </a>
      ) for cellulosic ethanol, corn ethanol, and gasoline are 10.3, 1.36, and 0.81, respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Ethanol has roughly one-third lower energy content per unit of volume compared to gasoline. This is partly counteracted by the better efficiency when using ethanol (in a long-term test of more than 2.1 million km, the BEST project found FFV vehicles to be 1-26 % more energy efficient than petrol cars
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.best-europe.org/" rel="nofollow">
       The BEST project
      </a>
      ), but the volumetric consumption increases by approximately 30%, so more fuel stops are required.
     </p>
     <p>
      With current subsidies, ethanol fuel is slightly cheaper per distance traveled in the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiesel">
       Biodiesel
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Biodiesel">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">
       Biodiesel
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodiesel_around_the_world" title="Biodiesel around the world">
       Biodiesel around the world
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">
       Biodiesel
      </a>
      is the most common biofuel in Europe. It is produced from oils or fats using
      <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">
       transesterification
      </a>
      and is a liquid similar in composition to fossil/mineral diesel. Chemically, it consists mostly of fatty acid methyl (or ethyl) esters (
      <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_methyl_ester" title="Fatty acid methyl ester">
       FAMEs
      </a>
      ). Feedstocks for biodiesel include animal fats, vegetable oils,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy" title="Soy">
       soy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Rapeseed" title="Rapeseed">
       rapeseed
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jatropha_curcas" title="Jatropha curcas">
       jatropha
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Madhuca_longifolia" title="Madhuca longifolia">
       mahua
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mustard_plant" title="Mustard plant">
       mustard
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">
       flax
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunflower" title="Sunflower">
       sunflower
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">
       palm oil
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">
       hemp
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Thlaspi_arvense" title="Thlaspi arvense">
       field pennycress
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pongamia_pinnata" title="Pongamia pinnata">
        Pongamia pinnata
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel" title="Algae fuel">
       algae
      </a>
      . Pure biodiesel (B100) currently reduces emissions with up to 60% compared to diesel
      <a class="external text" href="https://www.perstorp.com/en/Media/Pressreleases/2013/20130701_Verdis_Polaris_Aura_second_generation_B100/" rel="nofollow">
       Second generation B100
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Biodiesel can be used in any
      <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">
       diesel engine
      </a>
      when mixed with mineral diesel. In some countries, manufacturers cover their diesel engines under warranty for B100 use, although
      <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">
       Volkswagen
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
       Germany
      </a>
      , for example, asks drivers to check by telephone with the VW environmental services department before switching to B100. B100 may become more
      <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">
       viscous
      </a>
      at lower temperatures, depending on the feedstock used. In most cases, biodiesel is compatible with diesel engines from 1994 onwards, which use '
      <a href="/wiki/Viton" title="Viton">
       Viton
      </a>
      ' (by
      <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">
       DuPont
      </a>
      ) synthetic rubber in their mechanical
      <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection" title="Fuel injection">
       fuel injection
      </a>
      systems. Note however, that no vehicles are certified for using neat biodiesel before 2014, as there was no emission control protocol available for biodiesel before this date.
     </p>
     <p>
      Electronically controlled '
      <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">
       common rail
      </a>
      ' and '
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unit_Injector" title="Unit Injector">
       unit injector
      </a>
      ' type systems from the late 1990s onwards may only use biodiesel blended with conventional diesel fuel. These engines have finely metered and atomized multiple-stage injection systems that are very sensitive to the viscosity of the fuel. Many current-generation diesel engines are made so that they can run on B100 without altering the engine itself, although this depends on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_rail" title="Fuel rail">
       fuel rail
      </a>
      design. Since biodiesel is an effective
      <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">
       solvent
      </a>
      and cleans residues deposited by mineral diesel,
      <a href="/wiki/Oil_filter" title="Oil filter">
       engine filters
      </a>
      may need to be replaced more often, as the biofuel dissolves old deposits in the fuel tank and pipes. It also effectively cleans the engine
      <a href="/wiki/Combustion_chamber" title="Combustion chamber">
       combustion chamber
      </a>
      of carbon deposits, helping to maintain efficiency. In many European countries, a 5% biodiesel blend is widely used and is available at thousands of gas stations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Biodiesel is also an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygenated_diesel" title="Oxygenated diesel">
       oxygenated fuel
      </a>
      , meaning it contains a reduced amount of carbon and higher hydrogen and oxygen content than fossil diesel. This improves the
      <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">
       combustion
      </a>
      of biodiesel and reduces the particulate emissions from unburnt carbon. However, using neat biodiesel may increase NOx-emissions
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.vtt.fi/inf/pdf/technology/2012/T46.pdf" rel="nofollow">
       Nylund.N-O &amp; Koponen.K. 2013. Fuel and Technology Alternatives for Buses. Overall Energy Efficiency and Emission Performance. IEA Bioenergy Task 46
      </a>
      . Possibly the new emission standards Euro VI/EPA 10 will lead to reduced NOx-levels also when using B100.
     </p>
     <p>
      Biodiesel is also safe to handle and transport because it is non-toxic and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodegradable" title="Biodegradable">
       biodegradable
      </a>
      , and has a high
      <a href="/wiki/Flash_point" title="Flash point">
       flash point
      </a>
      of about 300 °F (148 °C) compared to petroleum diesel fuel, which has a flash point of 125 °F (52 °C).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the USA, more than 80% of commercial trucks and city buses run on diesel. The emerging US biodiesel market is estimated to have grown 200% from 2004 to 2005. "By the end of 2006 biodiesel production was estimated to increase fourfold [from 2004] to more than" 1 billion US gallons (3,800,000 m
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In France, biodiesel is incorporated at a rate of 8% in the fuel used by all French diesel vehicles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RA29_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RA29-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Avril_Group" title="Avril Group">
       Avril Group
      </a>
      produces under the brand
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diester" title="Diester">
       Diester
      </a>
      , a fifth of 11 million tons of biodiesel consumed annually by the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
       European Union
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EurObserv.E2.80.99ER_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EurObserv.E2.80.99ER-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is the leading European producer of biodiesel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RA29_16-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-RA29-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_bioalcohols">
       Other bioalcohols
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Other bioalcohols">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">
       Methanol
      </a>
      is currently produced from
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">
       natural gas
      </a>
      , a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-renewable" title="Non-renewable">
       non-renewable
      </a>
      fossil fuel. In the future it is hoped to be produced from biomass as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomethanol" title="Biomethanol">
       biomethanol
      </a>
      . This is technically feasible, but the production is currently being postponed for concerns of
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jacob_S._Gibbs&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jacob S. Gibbs (page does not exist)">
       Jacob S. Gibbs
      </a>
      and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Brinsley_Coleberd&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Brinsley Coleberd (page does not exist)">
       Brinsley Coleberd
      </a>
      that the economic viability is still pending.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Methanol_economy" title="Methanol economy">
       methanol economy
      </a>
      is an alternative to the
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">
       hydrogen economy
      </a>
      , compared to today's
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      production from natural gas.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Butanol_fuel" title="Butanol fuel">
       Butanol
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       C
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
       H
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        9
       </span>
       OH
      </span>
      ) is formed by
      <a href="/wiki/Clostridium_acetobutylicum" title="Clostridium acetobutylicum">
       ABE fermentation
      </a>
      (acetone, butanol, ethanol) and experimental modifications of the process show potentially high
      <a href="/wiki/Net_energy_gain" title="Net energy gain">
       net energy gains
      </a>
      with butanol as the only liquid product. Butanol will produce more energy and allegedly can be burned "straight" in existing gasoline engines (without modification to the engine or car),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and is less corrosive and less water-soluble than ethanol, and could be distributed via existing infrastructures.
      <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">
       DuPont
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">
       BP
      </a>
      are working together to help develop butanol.
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">
        E. coli
       </a>
      </i>
      strains have also been successfully engineered to produce butanol by modifying their
      <a href="/wiki/Protein_metabolism" title="Protein metabolism">
       amino acid metabolism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-butanol_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-butanol-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Green_diesel">
       Green diesel
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Green diesel">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_refining" title="Vegetable oil refining">
       Vegetable oil refining
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_refining" title="Vegetable oil refining">
       Green diesel
      </a>
      is produced through
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocracking" title="Hydrocracking">
       hydrocracking
      </a>
      biological oil feedstocks, such as vegetable oils and animal fats.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-seven-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hydrocracking is a refinery method that uses elevated temperatures and pressure in the presence of a catalyst to break down larger
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecules" title="Molecules">
       molecules
      </a>
      , such as those found in
      <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">
       vegetable oils
      </a>
      , into shorter
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">
       hydrocarbon
      </a>
      chains used in
      <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">
       diesel
      </a>
      engines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alpha_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-alpha-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It may also be called renewable diesel, hydrotreated vegetable oil
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alpha_23-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-alpha-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      or hydrogen-derived renewable diesel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-seven-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Green diesel has the same chemical properties as petroleum-based diesel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alpha_23-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-alpha-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It does not require new engines, pipelines or infrastructure to distribute and use, but has not been produced at a cost that is competitive with
      <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">
       petroleum
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_22-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-seven-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Gasoline versions are also being developed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Green diesel is being developed in
      <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">
       Louisiana
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">
       Singapore
      </a>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/ConocoPhillips" title="ConocoPhillips">
       ConocoPhillips
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neste_Oil" title="Neste Oil">
       Neste Oil
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valero_Energy_Corporation" title="Valero Energy Corporation">
       Valero
      </a>
      , Dynamic Fuels, and
      <a href="/wiki/UOP_LLC" title="UOP LLC">
       Honeywell UOP
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_22-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-seven-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      as well as Preem in Gothenburg, Sweden, creating what is known as
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.portofgothenburg.com/News-desk/Press-releases/Preem-makes-major-investment-in-green-diesel-at-the-Port-of-Gothenburg/" rel="nofollow">
       Evolution Diesel
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biofuel_gasoline">
       Biofuel gasoline
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Biofuel gasoline">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In 2013 UK researchers developed a genetically modified strain of
      <a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">
       Escherichia coli
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E.Coli" title="E.Coli">
       E.Coli
      </a>
      ), which could transform glucose into biofuel gasoline that does not need to be blended.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Later in 2013
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UCLA" title="UCLA">
       UCLA
      </a>
      researchers engineered a new metabolic pathway to bypass
      <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">
       glycolysis
      </a>
      and increase the rate of conversion of sugars into biofuel,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      while
      <a href="/wiki/KAIST" title="KAIST">
       KAIST
      </a>
      researchers developed a strain capable of producing short-chain alkanes, free fatty acids, fatty esters and fatty alcohols through the fatty acyl (acyl carrier protein (ACP)) to fatty acid to fatty acyl-CoA pathway
      <i>
       in vivo
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is believed that in the future it will be possible to "tweak" the genes to make gasoline from straw or animal manure.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Vegetable_oil">
       Vegetable oil
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Vegetable oil">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:167px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Used_vegetable_cooking_oil.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="165" height="413" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a9/Used_vegetable_cooking_oil.png" width="165"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Used_vegetable_cooking_oil.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Filtered waste vegetable oil
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_(2).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="2464" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg/220px-Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg/330px-Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg/440px-Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Walmart" title="Walmart">
         Walmart
        </a>
        's truck fleet logs millions of miles each year, and the company planned to double the fleet's efficiency between 2005 and 2015.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
         <a href="#cite_note-29">
          [29]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This truck is one of 15 based at Walmart's
        <a href="/wiki/Buckeye,_Arizona" title="Buckeye, Arizona">
         Buckeye, Arizona
        </a>
        distribution center that was converted to run on a biofuel made from reclaimed cooking grease produced during food preparation at Walmart stores.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
         <a href="#cite_note-30">
          [30]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_fuel" title="Vegetable oil fuel">
       Vegetable oil fuel
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Straight unmodified
      <a href="/wiki/Eating" title="Eating">
       edible
      </a>
      vegetable oil is generally not used as fuel, but lower-quality oil can and has been used for this purpose. Used vegetable oil is increasingly being processed into biodiesel, or (more rarely) cleaned of water and particulates and used as a fuel.
     </p>
     <p>
      As with 100% biodiesel (B100), to ensure the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_injector" title="Fuel injector">
       fuel injectors
      </a>
      atomize the vegetable oil in the correct pattern for efficient combustion,
      <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_fuel" title="Vegetable oil fuel">
       vegetable oil fuel
      </a>
      must be heated to reduce its
      <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">
       viscosity
      </a>
      to that of diesel, either by electric coils or heat exchangers. This is easier in warm or temperate climates.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MAN_B%26W_Diesel" title="MAN B&amp;W Diesel">
       MAN B&amp;W Diesel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4rtsil%C3%A4" title="Wärtsilä">
       Wärtsilä
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Deutz_AG" title="Deutz AG">
       Deutz AG
      </a>
      , as well as a number of smaller companies, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Elsbett" title="Elsbett">
       Elsbett
      </a>
      , offer engines that are compatible with straight vegetable oil, without the need for after-market modifications.
     </p>
     <p>
      Vegetable oil can also be used in many older diesel engines that do not use
      <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">
       common rail
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unit_Injector" title="Unit Injector">
       unit injection
      </a>
      electronic diesel injection systems. Due to the design of the combustion chambers in
      <a href="/wiki/Indirect_injection" title="Indirect injection">
       indirect injection
      </a>
      engines, these are the best engines for use with vegetable oil. This system allows the relatively larger oil molecules more time to burn. Some older engines, especially Mercedes, are driven experimentally by enthusiasts without any conversion, a handful of drivers have experienced limited success with earlier pre-"Pumpe Duse"
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VW_TDI" title="VW TDI">
       VW TDI
      </a>
      engines and other similar engines with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_fuel_injection" title="Direct fuel injection">
       direct injection
      </a>
      . Several companies, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Elsbett" title="Elsbett">
       Elsbett
      </a>
      or Wolf, have developed professional conversion kits and successfully installed hundreds of them over the last decades.
     </p>
     <p>
      Oils and fats can be
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">
       hydrogenated
      </a>
      to give a diesel substitute. The resulting product is a straight-chain hydrocarbon with a high
      <a href="/wiki/Cetane_number" title="Cetane number">
       cetane number
      </a>
      , low in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatics" title="Aromatics">
       aromatics
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">
       sulfur
      </a>
      and does not contain oxygen.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogenated_oil" title="Hydrogenated oil">
       Hydrogenated oils
      </a>
      can be blended with diesel in all proportions. They have several advantages over biodiesel, including good performance at low temperatures, no storage stability problems and no susceptibility to microbial attack.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evans_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-evans-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bioethers">
       Bioethers
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bioethers">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Bio
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethers" title="Ethers">
       ethers
      </a>
      (also referred to as fuel ethers or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygenated_fuel" title="Oxygenated fuel">
       oxygenated fuels
      </a>
      ) are cost-effective
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">
       compounds
      </a>
      that act as
      <a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">
       octane rating
      </a>
      enhancers."Bioethers are produced by the reaction of reactive iso-olefins, such as iso-butylene, with bioethanol."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Bioethers are created by wheat or sugar beet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      They also enhance
      <a href="/wiki/Engine" title="Engine">
       engine
      </a>
      performance, whilst significantly reducing engine wear and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">
       toxic
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_gas" title="Exhaust gas">
       exhaust emissions
      </a>
      . Though bioethers are likely to replace petroethers in the UK, it is highly unlikely they will become a fuel in and of itself due to the low energy density.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Greatly reducing the amount of ground-level
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_emissions&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ozone emissions (page does not exist)">
       ozone emissions
      </a>
      , they contribute to air quality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When it comes to transportation fuel there are six ether additives- 1. Dimethyl Ether (DME) 2. Diethyl Ether (DEE) 3. Methyl Teritiary-Butyl Ether (MTBE) 4. Ethyl
      <i>
       ter
      </i>
      -butyl ether (ETBE) 5. T
      <i>
       er
      </i>
      -amyl methyl ether (TAME) 6.
      <i>
       Ter
      </i>
      -amyl ethyl Ether (TAEE)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The European Fuel Oxygenates Association (aka EFOA) credits Methyl Tertiary-Butyl Ether (MTBE) and Ethyl ter-butyl ether (ETBE) as the most commonly used ethers in fuel to replace lead. Ethers were brought into fuels in Europe in the 1970s to replace the highly toxic compound.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although Europeans still use Bio-ether additives, the US no longer has an oxygenate requirement therefore bio-ethers are no longer used as the main fuel additive.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biogas">
       Biogas
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Biogas">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biogas_pipes.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Biogas_pipes.JPG/150px-Biogas_pipes.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Biogas_pipes.JPG/225px-Biogas_pipes.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Biogas_pipes.JPG/300px-Biogas_pipes.JPG 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biogas_pipes.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Pipes carrying biogas
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">
       Biogas
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Biogas is
      <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
       methane
      </a>
      produced by the process of
      <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion" title="Anaerobic digestion">
       anaerobic digestion
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">
       organic material
      </a>
      by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobe" title="Anaerobe">
       anaerobes
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It can be produced either from
      <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable_waste" title="Biodegradable waste">
       biodegradable waste
      </a>
      materials or by the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_crop" title="Energy crop">
       energy crops
      </a>
      fed into
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digester" title="Anaerobic digester">
       anaerobic digesters
      </a>
      to supplement gas yields. The solid byproduct,
      <a href="/wiki/Digestate" title="Digestate">
       digestate
      </a>
      , can be used as a biofuel or a fertilizer.
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Biogas can be recovered from
       <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_biological_treatment" title="Mechanical biological treatment">
        mechanical biological treatment
       </a>
       waste processing systems.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       Note:
       <a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">
        Landfill gas
       </a>
       , a less clean form of biogas, is produced in
       <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">
        landfills
       </a>
       through naturally occurring anaerobic digestion. If it escapes into the atmosphere, it is a potential
       <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">
        greenhouse gas
       </a>
       .
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Farmers can produce biogas from
       <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">
        manure
       </a>
       from their cattle by using anaerobic digesters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Syngas">
       Syngas
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Syngas">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">
       Gasification
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">
       Syngas
      </a>
      , a mixture of
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">
       carbon monoxide
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      and other hydrocarbons, is produced by partial combustion of biomass, that is, combustion with an amount of
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
       oxygen
      </a>
      that is not sufficient to convert the biomass completely to carbon dioxide and water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evans_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-evans-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before partial combustion, the biomass is dried, and sometimes
      <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">
       pyrolysed
      </a>
      . The resulting gas mixture, syngas, is more efficient than direct combustion of the original biofuel; more of the energy contained in the fuel is extracted.
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Syngas may be burned directly in internal combustion engines,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbines" title="Turbines">
        turbines
       </a>
       or high-temperature fuel cells.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-e-collection.ethbib.ethz.ch_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-e-collection.ethbib.ethz.ch-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Wood_gas_generator" title="Wood gas generator">
        wood gas generator
       </a>
       , a wood-fueled gasification reactor, can be connected to an internal combustion engine.
      </li>
      <li>
       Syngas can be used to produce
       <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">
        methanol
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_ether" title="Dimethyl ether">
        DME
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
        hydrogen
       </a>
       , or converted via the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" title="Fischer-Tropsch process">
        Fischer-Tropsch process
       </a>
       to produce a diesel substitute, or a mixture of alcohols that can be blended into gasoline. Gasification normally relies on temperatures greater than 700 °C.
      </li>
      <li>
       Lower-temperature gasification is desirable when co-producing
       <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">
        biochar
       </a>
       , but results in syngas polluted with
       <a href="/wiki/Tar" title="Tar">
        tar
       </a>
       .
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Solid_biofuels">
       Solid biofuels
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Solid biofuels">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Examples include
      <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">
       wood
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sawdust" title="Sawdust">
       sawdust
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass" title="Grass">
       grass
      </a>
      trimmings,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_waste" title="Domestic waste">
       domestic refuse
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">
       charcoal
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural_waste" title="Agricultural waste">
       agricultural waste
      </a>
      , nonfood
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_crop" title="Energy crop">
       energy crops
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Dry_animal_dung_fuel" title="Dry animal dung fuel">
       dried manure
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      When raw biomass is already in a suitable form (such as
      <a href="/wiki/Firewood" title="Firewood">
       firewood
      </a>
      ), it can burn directly in a stove or furnace to provide heat or raise steam. When raw biomass is in an inconvenient form (such as sawdust, wood chips, grass, urban waste wood, agricultural residues), the typical process is to densify the biomass. This process includes grinding the raw biomass to an appropriate particulate size (known as hogfuel), which, depending on the densification type, can be from 1 to 3 cm (0.4 to 1.2 in), which is then concentrated into a fuel product. The current processes produce
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_pellet" title="Wood pellet">
       wood pellets
      </a>
      , cubes, or pucks. The pellet process is most common in Europe, and is typically a pure wood product. The other types of densification are larger in size compared to a pellet, and are compatible with a broad range of input feedstocks. The resulting densified fuel is easier to transport and feed into thermal generation systems, such as boilers.
     </p>
     <p>
      Industry has used sawdust, bark and chips for fuel for decades, primary in the pulp and paper industry, and also bagasse (spent sugar cane) fueled boilers in the sugar cane industry. Boilers in the range of 500,000 lb/hr of steam, and larger, are in routine operation, using grate, spreader stoker, suspension burning and fluid bed combustion. Utilities generate power, typically in the range of 5 to 50 MW, using locally available fuel. Other industries have also installed wood waste fueled boilers and dryers in areas with low cost fuel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One of the advantages of biomass fuel is that it is often a byproduct, residue or waste-product of other processes, such as farming, animal husbandry and forestry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ODI1-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In theory, this means fuel and food production do not compete for resources, although this is not always the case.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ODI1-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A problem with the combustion of raw biomass is that it emits considerable amounts of
      <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">
       pollutants
      </a>
      , such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">
       particulates
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">
       polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons
      </a>
      . Even modern pellet boilers generate much more pollutants than oil or natural gas boilers. Pellets made from agricultural residues are usually worse than wood pellets, producing much larger emissions of
      <a href="/wiki/Dioxins_and_dioxin-like_compounds" title="Dioxins and dioxin-like compounds">
       dioxins
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Chlorophenol" title="Chlorophenol">
       chlorophenols
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In spite of the above noted study, numerous studies have shown biomass fuels have significantly less impact on the environment than fossil based fuels. Of note is the US Department of Energy Laboratory, operated by Midwest Research Institute Biomass Power and Conventional Fossil Systems with and without CO2 Sequestration – Comparing the
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_returned_on_energy_invested" title="Energy returned on energy invested">
       Energy Balance
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_Gas" title="Greenhouse Gas">
       Greenhouse Gas
      </a>
      Emissions and Economics Study. Power generation emits significant amounts of greenhouse gases (GHGs), mainly
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
       carbon dioxide
      </a>
      (CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ).
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CO2_sequestration" title="CO2 sequestration">
       Sequestering
      </a>
      CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      from the power plant
      <a href="/wiki/Flue_gas" title="Flue gas">
       flue gas
      </a>
      can significantly reduce the GHGs from the power plant itself, but this is not the total picture. CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      capture and sequestration consumes additional energy, thus lowering the plant's
      <a href="/wiki/Fuel_efficiency" title="Fuel efficiency">
       fuel-to-electricity efficiency
      </a>
      . To compensate for this, more fossil fuel must be procured and consumed to make up for lost capacity.
     </p>
     <p>
      Taking this into consideration, the
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">
       global warming potential
      </a>
      (GWP), which is a combination of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , methane (CH
      <sub>
       4
      </sub>
      ), and
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">
       nitrous oxide
      </a>
      (N
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      O) emissions, and energy balance of the system need to be examined using a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" title="Life cycle assessment">
       life cycle assessment
      </a>
      . This takes into account the upstream processes which remain constant after CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      sequestration, as well as the steps required for additional power generation. Firing biomass instead of coal led to a 148% reduction in GWP.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2016)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A derivative of solid biofuel is
      <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">
       biochar
      </a>
      , which is produced by biomass
      <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">
       pyrolysis
      </a>
      . Biochar made from agricultural waste can substitute for wood charcoal. As wood stock becomes scarce, this alternative is gaining ground. In eastern
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">
       Democratic Republic of Congo
      </a>
      , for example, biomass
      <a href="/wiki/Briquette" title="Briquette">
       briquettes
      </a>
      are being marketed as an alternative to charcoal to protect
      <a href="/wiki/Virunga_National_Park" title="Virunga National Park">
       Virunga National Park
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">
       deforestation
      </a>
      associated with
      <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">
       charcoal
      </a>
      production.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Second-generation_.28advanced.29_biofuels">
       Second-generation (advanced) biofuels
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Second-generation (advanced) biofuels">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Second-generation_biofuels" title="Second-generation biofuels">
       Second-generation biofuels
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Second generation biofuels, also known as advanced biofuels, are fuels that can be manufactured from various types of
      <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">
       biomass
      </a>
      . Biomass is a wide-ranging term meaning any source of organic carbon that is renewed rapidly as part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">
       carbon cycle
      </a>
      . Biomass is derived from plant materials but can also include animal materials.
     </p>
     <p>
      First generation biofuels are made from the
      <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">
       sugars
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">
       vegetable oils
      </a>
      found in arable crops, which can be easily extracted using conventional technology. In comparison, second generation biofuels are made from
      <a href="/wiki/Lignocellulosic_biomass" title="Lignocellulosic biomass">
       lignocellulosic biomass
      </a>
      or woody crops, agricultural residues or waste, which makes it harder to extract the required fuel. A series of physical and chemical treatments might be required to convert lignocellulosic biomass to liquid fuels suitable for transportation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainable_biofuels">
       Sustainable biofuels
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sustainable biofuels">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sustainable_biofuels" title="Sustainable biofuels">
       Sustainable biofuels
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Biofuels in the form of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_fuels" title="Liquid fuels">
       liquid fuels
      </a>
      derived from plant materials are entering the market, driven mainly by the perception that they reduce climate gas emissions, and also by factors such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_price_increases_since_2003" title="Oil price increases since 2003">
       oil price spikes
      </a>
      and the need for increased
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">
       energy security
      </a>
      . However, many of the biofuels that are currently being supplied have been criticised for their adverse impacts on the
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">
       natural environment
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">
       food security
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">
       land use
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roy_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-roy-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sol_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sol-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2008, the Nobel-prize winning chemist
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_J._Crutzen" title="Paul J. Crutzen">
       Paul J. Crutzen
      </a>
      published findings that the release of
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">
       nitrous oxide
      </a>
      (N
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      O) emissions in the production of biofuels means that overall they contribute more to global warming than the fossil fuels they replace.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The challenge is to support biofuel development, including the development of new
      <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">
       cellulosic technologies
      </a>
      , with responsible policies and economic instruments to help ensure that biofuel commercialization is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sustainable" title="Sustainable">
       sustainable
      </a>
      . Responsible commercialization of biofuels represents an opportunity to enhance sustainable economic prospects in Africa, Latin America and Asia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roy_49-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-roy-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sol_50-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-sol-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to the
      <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain_Institute" title="Rocky Mountain Institute">
       Rocky Mountain Institute
      </a>
      , sound biofuel production practices would not hamper food and fibre production, nor cause water or environmental problems, and would enhance soil fertility.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The selection of land on which to grow the feedstocks is a critical component of the ability of biofuels to deliver sustainable solutions. A key consideration is the minimisation of biofuel competition for prime cropland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-royal_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-royal-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biofuels_by_region">
       Biofuels by region
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Biofuels by region">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Biofuels_by_region" title="Biofuels by region">
       Biofuels by region
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodiesel_around_the_world" title="Biodiesel around the world">
       Biodiesel around the world
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:289px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3672" data-file-width="4896" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg/287px-Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg/431px-Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg/574px-Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg 2x" width="287"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Bio Diesel Powered Fast Attack Craft Of
        <a href="/wiki/Indian_Navy" title="Indian Navy">
         Indian Navy
        </a>
        patrolling during
        <a href="/wiki/International_Fleet_Review_2016" title="International Fleet Review 2016">
         IFR 2016
        </a>
        .The green bands on the vessels are indicative of the fact that the vessels are powered by
        <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">
         bio-diesel
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      There are international organizations such as IEA Bioenergy,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      established in 1978 by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">
       OECD
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">
       International Energy Agency
      </a>
      (IEA), with the aim of improving cooperation and information exchange between countries that have national programs in bioenergy research, development and deployment. The
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">
       UN
      </a>
      International Biofuels Forum is formed by
      <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
       Brazil
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
       China
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
       India
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">
       Pakistan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">
       South Africa
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">
       European Commission
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The world leaders in biofuel development and use are Brazil, the United States, France, Sweden and Germany. Russia also has 22% of world's forest,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and is a big biomass (solid biofuels) supplier. In 2010, Russian pulp and paper maker, Vyborgskaya Cellulose, said they would be producing pellets that can be used in heat and electricity generation from its plant in Vyborg by the end of the year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The plant will eventually produce about 900,000 tons of pellets per year, making it the largest in the world once operational.
     </p>
