     <p>
      Biofuels currently make up 3.1%
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      of the total road transport fuel in the UK or 1,440 million litres. By 2020, 10% of the energy used in UK road and rail transport must come from renewable sources – this is the equivalent of replacing 4.3 million tonnes of fossil oil each year. Conventional biofuels are likely to produce between 3.7 and 6.6% of the energy needed in road and rail transport, while
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_biofuels" title="Advanced biofuels">
       advanced biofuels
      </a>
      could meet up to 4.3% of the UK’s renewable transport fuel target by 2020.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nnfcc_adv_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nnfcc_adv-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Air_pollution">
       Air pollution
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Air pollution">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Biomass#Environmental_impact" title="Biomass">
       Biomass § Environmental impact
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel#Air_pollution" title="Ethanol fuel">
       Ethanol_fuel § Air_pollution
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Biofuels are different from fossil fuels in regard to greenhouse gases but are similar to fossil fuels in that biofuels contribute to
      <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">
       air pollution
      </a>
      . Burning produces airborne carbon
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particulate" title="Particulate">
       particulates
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">
       carbon monoxide
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">
       nitrous oxides
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The WHO estimates 3.7 million premature deaths worldwide in 2012 due to air pollution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Brazil burns significant amounts of ethanol biofuel. Gas
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromatograph" title="Chromatograph">
       chromatograph
      </a>
      studies were performed of ambient air in São Paulo, Brazil, and compared to Osaka, Japan, which does not burn ethanol fuel. Atmospheric Formaldehyde was 160% higher in Brazil, and Acetaldehyde was 260% higher.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Debates_regarding_the_production_and_use_of_biofuel">
       Debates regarding the production and use of biofuel
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Debates regarding the production and use of biofuel">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Issues_relating_to_biofuels" title="Issues relating to biofuels">
       Issues relating to biofuels
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      There are various social, economic, environmental and technical issues with biofuel production and use, which have been discussed in the popular media and scientific journals. These include: the effect of moderating
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Price_of_petroleum" title="Price of petroleum">
       oil prices
      </a>
      , the "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_vs_fuel" title="Food vs fuel">
       food vs fuel
      </a>
      " debate,
      <a href="/wiki/Food_prices" title="Food prices">
       food prices
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Poverty_reduction" title="Poverty reduction">
       poverty reduction
      </a>
      potential,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_ratio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Energy ratio (page does not exist)">
       energy ratio
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_requirements&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Energy requirements (page does not exist)">
       energy requirements
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions">
       carbon emissions
      </a>
      levels,
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_biofuel" title="Sustainable biofuel">
       sustainable biofuel
      </a>
      production,
      <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">
       deforestation
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">
       soil erosion
      </a>
      , loss of
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">
       biodiversity
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      impact on
      <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">
       water resources
      </a>
      , the possible modifications necessary to run the engine on biofuel, as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_returned_on_energy_invested" title="Energy returned on energy invested">
       energy balance
      </a>
      and efficiency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">
       International Resource Panel
      </a>
      , which provides independent scientific assessments and expert advice on a variety of resource-related themes, assessed the issues relating to biofuel use in its first report
      <i>
       Towards sustainable production and use of resources: Assessing Biofuels
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      "Assessing Biofuels" outlined the wider and interrelated factors that need to be considered when deciding on the relative merits of pursuing one biofuel over another. It concluded that not all biofuels perform equally in terms of their impact on climate, energy security and ecosystems, and suggested that environmental and social impacts need to be assessed throughout the entire life-cycle.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another issue with biofuel use and production is the US has changed mandates many times because the production has been taking longer than expected. The Renewable Fuel Standard (RFS) set by congress for 2010 was pushed back to at best 2012 to produce 100 million gallons of pure ethanol (not blended with a fossil fuel).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Current_research">
       Current research
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Current research">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Research is ongoing into finding more suitable biofuel crops and improving the oil yields of these crops. Using the current yields, vast amounts of land and fresh water would be needed to produce enough oil to completely replace fossil fuel usage. It would require twice the land area of the US to be devoted to soybean production, or two-thirds to be devoted to rapeseed production, to meet current US heating and transportation needs.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2007)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Specially bred mustard varieties can produce reasonably high oil yields and are very useful in
      <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">
       crop rotation
      </a>
      with cereals, and have the added benefit that the meal left over after the oil has been pressed out can act as an effective and biodegradable pesticide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NFESC" title="NFESC">
       NFESC
      </a>
      , with
      <a href="/wiki/Santa_Barbara,_California" title="Santa Barbara, California">
       Santa Barbara
      </a>
      -based Biodiesel Industries, is working to develop biofuels technologies for the US navy and military, one of the largest diesel fuel users in the world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A group of Spanish developers working for a company called
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ecofasa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ecofasa (page does not exist)">
       Ecofasa
      </a>
      announced a new biofuel made from trash. The fuel is created from general urban waste which is treated by bacteria to produce fatty acids, which can be used to make biofuels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ethanol_biofuels">
       Ethanol biofuels
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Ethanol biofuels">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">
       Ethanol fuel
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol_commercialization" title="Cellulosic ethanol commercialization">
       Cellulosic ethanol commercialization
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      As the primary source of biofuels in North America, many organizations are conducting research in the area of
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">
       ethanol
      </a>
      production. The National Corn-to-Ethanol Research Center (NCERC) is a research division of
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Illinois_University_Edwardsville" title="Southern Illinois University Edwardsville">
       Southern Illinois University Edwardsville
      </a>
      dedicated solely to ethanol-based biofuel research projects.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the federal level, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USDA" title="USDA">
       USDA
      </a>
      conducts a large amount of research regarding ethanol production in the United States. Much of this research is targeted toward the effect of ethanol production on domestic food markets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A division of the U.S.
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">
       Department of Energy
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory_(NREL)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL) (page does not exist)">
       National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL)
      </a>
      , has also conducted various ethanol research projects, mainly in the area of cellulosic ethanol.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol_commercialization" title="Cellulosic ethanol commercialization">
       Cellulosic ethanol commercialization
      </a>
      is the process of building an industry out of methods of turning cellulose-containing organic matter into fuel. Companies, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iogen_Corp." title="Iogen Corp.">
       Iogen
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/POET" title="POET">
       POET
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Abengoa" title="Abengoa">
       Abengoa
      </a>
      , are building refineries that can process
      <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">
       biomass
      </a>
      and turn it into
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioethanol" title="Bioethanol">
       bioethanol
      </a>
      . Companies, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diversa" title="Diversa">
       Diversa
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Novozymes" title="Novozymes">
       Novozymes
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dyadic_International&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dyadic International (page does not exist)">
       Dyadic
      </a>
      , are producing enzymes that could enable a
      <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">
       cellulosic ethanol
      </a>
      future. The shift from food crop feedstocks to waste residues and native grasses offers significant opportunities for a range of players, from farmers to biotechnology firms, and from project developers to investors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As of 2013, the first commercial-scale plants to produce cellulosic biofuels have begun operating. Multiple pathways for the conversion of different biofuel feedstocks are being used. In the next few years, the cost data of these technologies operating at commercial scale, and their relative performance, will become available. Lessons learnt will lower the costs of the industrial processes involved.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In parts of Asia and Africa where drylands prevail,
      <a href="/wiki/Sweet_sorghum" title="Sweet sorghum">
       sweet sorghum
      </a>
      is being investigated as a potential source of food, feed and fuel combined. The crop is particularly suitable for growing in arid conditions, as it only extracts one seventh of the water used by sugarcane. In
      <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
       India
      </a>
      , and other places, sweet sorghum stalks are used to produce biofuel by squeezing the juice and then fermenting into
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">
       ethanol
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A study by researchers at the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Crops_Research_Institute_for_the_Semi-Arid_Tropics" title="International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics">
       International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICRISAT" title="ICRISAT">
       ICRISAT
      </a>
      ) found that growing sweet sorghum instead of grain sorghum could increase farmers incomes by US$40 per hectare per crop because it can provide fuel in addition to food and animal feed. With grain sorghum currently grown on over 11 million hectares (ha) in Asia and on 23.4 million ha in Africa, a switch to sweet sorghum could have a considerable economic impact.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Algae_biofuels">
       Algae biofuels
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Algae biofuels">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Algaculture" title="Algaculture">
       Algaculture
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel" title="Algae fuel">
       Algae fuel
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      From 1978 to 1996, the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">
       US NREL
      </a>
      experimented with using algae as a biofuels source in the "
      <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_Species_Program" title="Aquatic Species Program">
       Aquatic Species Program
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NREL_biofuels_algae_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NREL_biofuels_algae-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A self-published article by Michael Briggs, at the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_New_Hampshire" title="University of New Hampshire">
       UNH
      </a>
      Biofuels Group, offers estimates for the realistic replacement of all
      <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle" title="Motor vehicle">
       vehicular
      </a>
      fuel with biofuels by using algae that have a natural oil content greater than 50%, which Briggs suggests can be grown on algae ponds at
      <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">
       wastewater treatment
      </a>
      plants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briggs2004_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Briggs2004-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This oil-rich algae can then be extracted from the system and processed into biofuels, with the dried remainder further reprocessed to create ethanol. The production of algae to harvest oil for biofuels has not yet been undertaken on a commercial scale, but
      <a href="/wiki/Feasibility_study" title="Feasibility study">
       feasibility studies
      </a>
      have been conducted to arrive at the above yield estimate. In addition to its projected high yield, algaculture — unlike
      <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">
       crop-based
      </a>
      biofuels — does not entail a decrease in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_production" title="Food production">
       food production
      </a>
      , since it requires neither
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farmland_(farming)" title="Farmland (farming)">
       farmland
      </a>
      nor
      <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">
       fresh water
      </a>
      . Many companies are pursuing algae bioreactors for various purposes, including scaling up biofuels production to commercial levels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Prof.
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rodrigo_E._Teixeira&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rodrigo E. Teixeira (page does not exist)">
       Rodrigo E. Teixeira
      </a>
      from the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama_in_Huntsville" title="University of Alabama in Huntsville">
       University of Alabama in Huntsville
      </a>
      demonstrated the extraction of biofuels lipids from wet algae using a simple and economical reaction in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionic_liquids" title="Ionic liquids">
       ionic liquids
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Jatropha">
       Jatropha
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Jatropha">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Jatropha_curcas" title="Jatropha curcas">
       Jatropha curcas
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Several groups in various sectors are conducting research on
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Jatropha_curcas" title="Jatropha curcas">
        Jatropha curcas
       </a>
      </i>
      , a poisonous shrub-like tree that produces seeds considered by many to be a viable source of biofuels feedstock oil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Much of this research focuses on improving the overall per acre oil yield of Jatropha through advancements in genetics, soil science, and horticultural practices.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/SG_Biofuels" title="SG Biofuels">
       SG Biofuels
      </a>
      , a San Diego-based jatropha developer, has used molecular breeding and biotechnology to produce elite hybrid seeds that show significant yield improvements over first-generation varieties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      SG Biofuels also claims additional benefits have arisen from such strains, including improved flowering synchronicity, higher resistance to pests and diseases, and increased cold-weather tolerance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Plant Research International, a department of the
      <a href="/wiki/Wageningen_University_and_Research_Centre" title="Wageningen University and Research Centre">
       Wageningen University and Research Centre
      </a>
      in the Netherlands, maintains an ongoing Jatropha Evaluation Project that examines the feasibility of large-scale jatropha cultivation through field and laboratory experiments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Center for Sustainable Energy Farming (CfSEF) is a Los Angeles-based nonprofit research organization dedicated to jatropha research in the areas of plant science, agronomy, and horticulture. Successful exploration of these disciplines is projected to increase jatropha farm production yields by 200-300% in the next 10 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fungi">
       Fungi
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Fungi">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A group at the
      <a href="/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Russian Academy of Sciences">
       Russian Academy of Sciences
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">
       Moscow
      </a>
      , in a 2008 paper, stated they had isolated large amounts of lipids from single-celled fungi and turned it into biofuels in an economically efficient manner. More research on this fungal species,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Cunninghamella" title="Cunninghamella">
        Cunninghamella
       </a>
      </i>
      <i>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cunninghamella_japonica&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cunninghamella japonica (page does not exist)">
        japonica
       </a>
      </i>
      , and others, is likely to appear in the near future.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The recent discovery of a variant of the fungus
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gliocladium_roseum" title="Gliocladium roseum">
        Gliocladium roseum
       </a>
      </i>
      (later renamed Ascocoryne sarcoides) points toward the production of so-called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myco-diesel" title="Myco-diesel">
       myco-diesel
      </a>
      from cellulose. This organism was recently discovered in the rainforests of northern
      <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">
       Patagonia
      </a>
      , and has the unique capability of converting cellulose into medium-length hydrocarbons typically found in diesel fuel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many other fungi that can degrade cellulose and other polymers have been observed to produce molecules that are currently being engineered using organisms from other kingdoms, suggesting that fungi may play a large role in the bio-production of fuels in the future (reviewed in
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_gut_bacteria">
       Animal gut bacteria
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Animal gut bacteria">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Microbial
      <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">
       gastrointestinal flora
      </a>
      in a variety of animals have shown potential for the production of biofuels. Recent research has shown that TU-103, a strain of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Clostridium" title="Clostridium">
        Clostridium
       </a>
      </i>
      bacteria found in Zebra feces, can convert nearly any form of cellulose into butanol fuel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Microbes in panda waste are being investigated for their use in creating
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels" title="Biofuels">
       biofuels
      </a>
      from bamboo and other plant materials.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There has also been substantial research into the technology of using the gut microbiomes of wood-feeding insects for the conversion of lignocellulotic material into biofuel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gas_emissions">
       Greenhouse gas emissions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gas emissions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Some scientists have expressed concerns about land-use change in response to greater demand for crops to use for biofuel and the subsequent carbon emissions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Science-95">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The payback period, that is, the time it will take biofuels to pay back the carbon debt they acquire due to land-use change, has been estimated to be between 100 and 1000 years, depending on the specific instance and location of land-use change. However, no-till practices combined with cover-crop practices can reduce the payback period to three years for grassland conversion and 14 years for forest conversion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ES.26T_96-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ES.26T-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A study conducted in the Tocantis State, in northern Brazil, found that many families were cutting down forests in order to produce two conglomerates of oilseed plants, the J. curcas (JC group) and the R. communis (RC group). This region is composed of 15% Amazonian rainforest with high biodiversity, and 80% Cerrado forest with lower biodiversity. During the study, the farmers that planted the JC group released over 2193 Mg CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , while losing 53-105 Mg CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      sequestration from deforestation; and the RC group farmers released 562 Mg CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , while losing 48-90 Mg CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      to be sequestered from forest depletion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The production of these types of biofuels not only led into an increased emission of carbon dioxide, but also to lower efficiency of forests to absorb the gases that these farms were emitting. This has to do with the amount of fossil fuel the production of fuel crops involves. In addition, the intensive use of monocropping agriculture requires large amounts of water irrigation, as well as of fertilizers, herbicides and pesticides. This does not only lead to poisonous chemicals to disperse on water runoff, but also to the emission of nitrous oxide (NO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ) as a fertilizer byproduct, which is three hundred times more efficient in producing a greenhouse effect than carbon dioxide (CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Converting rainforests, peatlands, savannas, or grasslands to produce food crop–based biofuels in Brazil, Southeast Asia, and the United States creates a “biofuel carbon debt” by releasing 17 to 420 times more CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      than the annual greenhouse gas (GHG) reductions that these biofuels would provide by displacing fossil fuels. Biofuels made from waste biomass or from biomass grown on abandoned agricultural lands incur little to no carbon debt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science2_99-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Science2-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Water_Use">
       Water Use
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Water Use">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In addition to water required to grow crops, biofuel facilities require significant process water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaterI_100-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WaterI-100">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

         Alves Finco, Marcus V., and Werner Doppler. “Bioenergy and Sustainable Development: The Dilemma of Food Security and Climate Change in the Brazilian Savannah.” Energy for Sustainable Development 12 (2010): 194-199. Accessed October 30, 2014. doi: 10.1016/j.esd.2010.04.006
        </span>
       </li>
